Preface
Some time ago Charles B. Morrey and I wrote A First Course in Real Analy-
sis, a book that provides material sufï¬cient for a comprehensive one-year
course in analysis for those students who have completed a standard ele-
mentary course in calculus. The book has been through two editions, the
second of which appeared in 1991; small changes and corrections of mis-
prints have been made in the ï¬fth printing of the second edition, which
appeared recently.
However, for many students of mathematics and for those students
who intend to study any of the physical sciences and computer science,
the need is for a short one-semester course in real analysis rather than
a lengthy, detailed, comprehensive treatment. To ï¬ll this need the book
Basic Elements of Real Analysis provides, in a brief and elementary way,
the most important topics in the subject.
The ï¬rst chapter, which deals with the real number system, gives the
reader the opportunity to develop facility in proving elementary theo-
rems. Since most students who take this course have spent their efforts in
developing manipulative skills, such an introduction presents a welcome
change. The last section of this chapter, which establishes the technique
of mathematical induction, is especially helpful for those who have not
previously been exposed to this important topic.
Chapters 2 through 5 cover the theory of elementary calculus, includ-
ing differentiation and integration. Many of the theorems that are â€œstated
without proofâ€ in elementary calculus are proved here.
It is important to note that both the Darboux integral and the Riemann
integral are described thoroughly in Chapter 5 of this volume. Here we
vii

viii
Preface
establish the equivalence of these integrals, thus giving the reader insight
into what integration is all about.
For topics beyond calculus, the concept of a metric space is crucial.
Chapter 6 describes topology in metric spaces as well as the notion of
compactness, especially with regard to the Heineâ€“Borel theorem.
The subject of metric spaces leads in a natural way to the calculus
of functions in N-dimensional spaces with N > 2. Here derivatives of
functions of N variables are developed, and the Darboux and Riemann
integrals, as described in Chapter 5, are extended in Chapter 7 to N-
dimensional space.
Inï¬nite series is the subject of Chapter 8. After a review of the usual
tests for convergence and divergence of series, the emphasis shifts to
uniform convergence. The reader must master this concept in order to
understand the underlying ideas of both power series and Fourier se-
ries. Although Fourier series are not included in this text, the reader
should ï¬nd it fairly easy reading once he or she masters uniform con-
vergence. For those interested in studying computer science, not only
Fourier series but also the application of Fourier series to wavelet theory is
recommended. (See, e.g., Ten Lectures on Wavelets by Ingrid Daubechies.)
There are many important functions that are deï¬ned by integrals, the
integration taken over a ï¬nite interval, a half-inï¬nite integral, or one
from âˆ’âˆto +âˆ. An example is the well-known Gamma function. In
Chapter 9 we develop the necessary techniques for differentiation under
the integral sign of such functions (the Leibniz rule). Although desirable,
this chapter is optional, since the results are not used later in the text.
Chapter 10 treats the Riemannâ€“Stieltjes integral. After an introduction
to functions of bounded variation, we deï¬ne the R-S integral and show
how the usual integration-by-parts formula is a special case of this inte-
gral. The generality of the Riemannâ€“Stieltjes integral is further illustrated
by the fact that an inï¬nite series can always be considered as a special
case of a Riemannâ€“Stieltjes integral.
A subject that is heavily used in both pure and applied mathematics
is the Lagrange multiplier rule. In most cases this rule is stated without
proof but with applications discussed. However, we establish the rule
in Chapter 11 (Theorem 11.4) after developing the facts on the implicit
function theorem needed for the proof.
In the twelfth, and last, chapter we discuss vector functions in RN. We
prove the theorems of Green and Stokes and the divergence theorem,
not in full generality but of sufï¬cient scope for most applications. The
ambitious reader can get a more general insight either by referring to the
book A First Course in Real Analysis or the text Principles of Mathematical
Analysis by Walter Rudin.
Murray H. Protter
Berkeley, CA

The Real Number
System
1.1
Axioms for a Field
In this and the next four chapters we give a reasonably rigorous founda-
tion to the processes of calculus of functions of one variable, a subject
usually studied in a ï¬rst course in calculus. Calculus depends on the
properties of the real number system. To give a complete foundation for
calculus we would have to develop the real number system from the be-
ginning. Since such a development is lengthy and would divert us from
our goal of presenting a course in analysis, we suppose that the reader is
familiar with the usual properties of real numbers.
In this section we present a set of axioms that form a logical basis for
those processes of elementary algebra upon which calculus is based. Any
collection of objects satisfying the axioms given below is called a ï¬eld.
In particular, the system of real numbers satisï¬es these axioms, and we
indicate how the customary laws of elementary algebra concerning addi-
tion, subtraction, multiplication, and division are direct consequences of
the axioms for a ï¬eld.
Throughout the book we use the word equals or its symbol  to stand
for the words â€œis the same as.â€ The reader should compare this with other
uses for the symbol  such as that in plane geometry when, for example,
two line segments are said to be equal if they have the same length.
1

2
1.
The Real Number System
Axioms of Addition
A-1. Closure property
If a and b are numbers, there is one and only one number, denoted a + b,
called their sum.
A-2. Commutative law
For any two numbers a and b, the equality
b + a  a + b
holds.
A-3. Associative law
For all numbers a, b, and c, the equality
(a + b) + c  a + (b + c)
holds.
A-4. Existence of a zero
There is one and only one number 0, called zero, such that a + 0  a for any
number a.
A-5. Existence of a negative
If a is any number, there is one and only one number x such that a + x  0.
This number is called the negative of a and is denoted by âˆ’a.
Theorem 1.1
If a and b are any numbers, then there is one and only one number x such
that a + x  b. This number x is given by x  b + ( âˆ’a).
Proof
We must establish two results: (i) that b + (âˆ’a) satisï¬es the equation
a + x  b and (ii) that no other number satisï¬es this equation. To prove
(i), suppose that x  b + (âˆ’a). Then, using Axioms A-2 through A-4, we
see that
a + x  a + [b + (âˆ’a)]  a + [(âˆ’a) + b]  [a + (âˆ’a)] + b  0 + b  b.
Therefore, (i) holds. To prove (ii), suppose that x is some number such
that a + x  b. Adding (âˆ’a) to both sides of this equation, we ï¬nd that
(a + x) + (âˆ’a)  b + (âˆ’a).
Now,
(a + x) + (âˆ’a)  a + [x + (âˆ’a)]  a + [(âˆ’a) + x]
 [a + (âˆ’a)] + x  0 + x  x.

Axioms of Multiplication
M-1. Closure property
If a and b are numbers, there is one and only one number, denoted by ab (or
a Ã— b or a Â· b), called their product.
M-2. Commutative law
For every two numbers a and b, the equality
ba  ab
holds.
M-3. Associative law
For all numbers a, b, and c, the equality
(ab)c  a(bc)
holds.

4
1.
The Real Number System
M-4. Existence of a unit
There is one and only one number u, different from zero, such that au  a
for every number a. This number u is called the unit and (as is customary)
is denoted by 1.
M-5. Existence of a reciprocal
For each number a different from zero there is one and only one number x
such that ax  1. This number x is called the reciprocal of a (or the inverse
of a) and is denoted by aâˆ’1 (or 1/a).
Axioms M-1 through M-4 are the parallels of Axioms A-1 through A-4
with addition replaced by multiplication. However, M-5 is not the exact
analogue of A-5, since the additional condition a Ì¸ 0 is required. The
reason for this is given below in Theorem 1.3, where it is shown that the
result of multiplication of any number by zero is zero. We are familiar
with the fact that says that division by zero is excluded.
Special Axiom on distributivity
D. Distributive law
For all numbers a, b, and c, the equality
a(b + c)  ab + ac
holds.
In every logical system there are certain terms that are undeï¬ned. For
example, in the system of axioms for plane Euclidean geometry, the terms
point and line are undeï¬ned. Of course, we have an intuitive idea of the
meaning of these two undeï¬ned terms, but in the framework of Euclidean
geometry it is not possible to deï¬ne them. In the axioms for algebra given
above, the term number is undeï¬ned. We shall interpret number to mean
real number (positive, negative, or zero) in the usual sense that we give
to it in elementary courses. Actually, the above axioms for a ï¬eld hold
for many systems, of which the collection of real numbers is only one.
For example, all the axioms stated so far hold for the system consisting
of all complex numbers. Also, there are many systems, each consisting of
a ï¬nite number of elements (ï¬nite ï¬elds), that satisfy all the axioms we
have stated until now.
Additional axioms are needed if we insist that the real number system
be the only collection satisfying all the given axioms. The additional axiom
required for this purpose is discussed in Section 1.3.
Theorem 1.3
If a is any number, then a Â· 0  0.

Notation. The expression â€œif and only if,â€ a technical one used fre-
quently in mathematics, requires some explanation. Suppose A and B
stand for propositions that may be true or false. To say that A is true if B
is true means that the truth of B implies the truth of A. The statement A is
true only if B is true means that the truth of A implies the truth of B. Thus
the shorthand statement â€œA is true if and only if B is trueâ€ is equivalent
to the double implication that the truth of A implies and is implied by the
truth of B. As a further shorthand we use the symbol â‡”to represent â€œif
and only if,â€ and we write
A â‡”B
for the two implications stated above. The term necessary and sufï¬cient is
used as a synonym for â€œif and only if.â€
We now establish the familiar principle that is the basis for the solution

Theorem 1.5
(i) We have ab  0 if and only if a  0 or b  0 or both.
(ii) We have a Ì¸ 0 and b Ì¸ 0 if and only if ab Ì¸ 0.

are two cases: either a  0 or a Ì¸ 0. If a  0, the result follows. If a Ì¸ 0,
then we see that

b Ì¸ 0. Then ab Ì¸ 0, because a Ì¸ 0 and b Ì¸ 0 is the negation of the

Using Theorem 1.3 and the distributive law, we easily prove the laws

Theorem 1.7

We now show that the laws of fractions, as given in elementary algebra,

Notation. We introduce the following standard symbols for a Â· bâˆ’1:

1.2.
Natural Numbers and Sequences

1.2
Natural Numbers and Sequences
Traditionally we build the real number system by a sequence of enlarge-
ments. We start with the positive integers and extend that system to
include the positive rational numbers (quotients, or ratios, of integers).
The system of rational numbers is then enlarged to include all positive

8
1.
The Real Number System
real numbers; ï¬nally we adjoin the negative numbers and zero to obtain
the collection of all real numbers.
The system of axioms in Section 1.1 does not distinguish (or even
mention) positive numbers. To establish the relationship between these
axioms and the real number system, we begin with a discussion of
natural numbers. As we know, these are the same as the positive
integers.
We can obtain the totality of natural numbers by starting with the num-
ber 1 and then forming 1 + 1, (1 + 1) + 1, [(1 + 1) + 1] + 1, and so on. We
call 1 + 1 the number 2; then (1 + 1) + 1 is called the number 3; in this
way the collection of natural numbers is generated. Actually it is possible
to give an abstract deï¬nition of natural number, one that yields the same
collection and is logically more satisfactory. This is done in Section 1.4,
where the principle of mathematical induction is established and illus-
trated. Meanwhile, we shall suppose that the reader is familiar with all
the usual properties of natural numbers.
The axioms for a ï¬eld given in Section 1.1 determine addition and
multiplication for any two numbers. On the basis of these axioms we were
able to deï¬ne the sum and product of three numbers. Before describing
the process of deï¬ning sums and products for more than three elements,
we now recall several deï¬nitions and give some notations that will be
used throughout the book.
Deï¬nitions
The set (or collection) of all real numbers is denoted by R1. The set of
ordered pairs of real numbers is denoted by R2, the set of ordered triples
by R3, and so on. A relation from R1 to R1 is a set of ordered pairs of real
numbers; that is, a relation from R1 to R1 is a set in R2. The domain of this
relation is the set in R1 consisting of all the ï¬rst elements in the ordered
pairs. The range of the relation is the set of all the second elements in
the ordered pairs. Observe that the range is also a set in R1.
A function f from R1 into R1 is a relation in which no two ordered
pairs have the same ï¬rst element. We use the notation f : R1 â†’R1 for
such a function. The word mapping is a synonym for function.
If D is the domain of f and S is its range, we shall also use the notation
f : D â†’S. A function is a relation (set in R2) such that for each element
x in the domain there is precisely one element y in the range such that
the pair (x, y) is one of the ordered pairs that constitute the function.
Occasionally a function will be indicated by writing f : x â†’y. Also, for
a given function f , the unique number in the range corresponding to an
element x in the domain in written f(x). The symbol x â†’f(x) is used for
this relationship. We assume that the reader is familiar with functional
notation.
A sequence is a function that has as its domain some or all of the
natural numbers. If the domain consists of a ï¬nite number of positive

1.2.
Natural Numbers and Sequences
9
integers, we say that the sequence is ï¬nite. Otherwise, the sequence is
called inï¬nite. In general, the elements in the domain of a function do
not have any particular order. In a sequence, however, there is a natural
ordering of the domain induced by the usual order in terms of size that
we give to the positive integers. For example, if the domain of a sequence
consists of the numbers 1, 2, . . . , n, then the elements of the range, that
is, the terms of the sequence, are usually written in the same order as
the natural numbers. If the sequence (function) is denoted by a, then
the terms of the sequence are denoted by a1, a2, . . . , an or, sometimes
by a(1), a(2), . . . , a(n). The element ai or a(i) is called the ith term of the
sequence. If the domain of a sequence a is the set of all natural numbers
(so that the sequence is inï¬nite), we denote the sequence by
a1, a2, . . . , an, . . .
or {an}.

The decimal system of writing numbers (or the system with any base)
depends on a representation theorem that we now state. If n is any natural
number, then there is one and only one representation for n of the form
n  d0(10)k + d1(10)kâˆ’1 + Â· Â· Â· + dkâˆ’1(10) + dk

the decimal system. On the basis of such a representation, the rules of
arithmetic follow from the corresponding rules for polynomials in x with
x  10.
For completeness, we deï¬ne the terms integer, rational number, and
irrational number.
Deï¬nitions
A real number is an integer if and only if it is either zero, a natural
number, or the negative of a natural number. A real number r is said to
be rational if and only if there are integers p and q, with q Ì¸ 0, such that
r  p/q. A real number that is not rational is called irrational.
It is clear that the sum and product of a ï¬nite sequence of integers
is again an integer, and that the sum, product, or quotient of a ï¬nite
sequence of rational numbers is a rational number.
The rule for multiplication of fractions is given by an extension of
Theorem 1.7 that may be derived by mathematical induction.
We emphasize that the axioms for a ï¬eld given in Section 1.1 imply
only theorems concerned with the operations of addition, subtraction,
multiplication, and division. The exact nature of the elements in the ï¬eld
is not described. For example, the axioms do not imply the existence of a
number whose square is 2. In fact, if we interpret number to be â€œrational
numberâ€ and consider no others, then all the axioms for a ï¬eld are sat-
isï¬ed. The rational number system forms a ï¬eld. An additional axiom is
needed if we wish the ï¬eld to contain irrational numbers such as

1.3
Inequalities
The axioms for a ï¬eld describe many number systems. If we wish to
describe the real number system to the exclusion of other systems, ad-
ditional axioms are needed. One of these, an axiom that distinguishes
positive from negative numbers, is the Axiom of inequality.

12
1.
The Real Number System
Axiom I
(Axiom of inequality) Among all the numbers of the system, there is a set
called the positive numbers that satisï¬es the two conditions: (i) for any number
a exactly one of the following three alternatives holds: a is positive or a  0 or
âˆ’a is positive; (ii) any ï¬nite sum or product of positive numbers is positive.
When we add Axiom I to those of Section 1.1, the resulting system of
axioms is applicable only to those number systems that have a linear or-
der. For example, the system of complex numbers does not satisfy Axiom
I but does satisfy all the axioms for a ï¬eld. Similarly, it is easy to see that
the system described in Problem 6 of Section 1.2 does not satisfy Axiom I.
However, both the real number system and the rational number system
satisfy all the axioms given thus far.
Deï¬nition
A number a is negative whenever âˆ’a is positive. If a and b are any
numbers, we say that a > b (read: a is greater than b) whenever a âˆ’b is
positive.
It is convenient to adopt a geometric point of view and to associate
a horizontal axis with the totality of real numbers. We select any conve-
nient point for the origin and call points to the right of the origin positive
numbers and points to the left negative numbers (Figure 1.1). For every
real number there will correspond a point on the line, and conversely,
every point will represent a real number. Then the inequality a < b may
be read: a is to the left of b. This geometric way of looking at inequalities
is frequently of help in solving problems.
Figure 1.1
It is helpful to introduce the notion of an interval of numbers or points.
If a and b are numbers (as shown in Figure 1.2), then the open interval
from a to b is the collection of all numbers that are both larger than a and
smaller than b. That is, an open interval consists of all numbers between
a and b. A number x is between a and b if both inequalities a < x and
x < b are true. A compact way of writing these two inequalities is
a < x < b.
The closed interval from a to b consists of all the points between a and
b and in addition the numbers (or points) a and b (Figure 1.3).

1.3.
Inequalities

Figure 1.3

greater than or equal to a. Similarly, x â‰¤b is read: x is less than or equal
to b, and means that x may be either smaller than b or may be b itself. A
compact way of designating all points x in the closed interval from a to b
is
a â‰¤x â‰¤b.
An interval that contains the endpoint b but not a is said to be half-open
on the left. Such an interval consists of all points x that satisfy the double
inequality
a < x â‰¤b.
Similarly, an interval containing a but not b is called half-open on the
right, and we write
a â‰¤x < b.
Parentheses and brackets are used as symbols for intervals in the
following way:
(a, b)
for the open interval a < x < b,
[a, b]
for the closed interval a â‰¤x â‰¤b,
(a, b]
for the interval half-open on the left a < x â‰¤b,
[a, b)
for the interval half-open on the right a â‰¤x < b.
We extend the idea of interval to include the unbounded cases. For exam-
ple, consider the set of all numbers larger than 7. This set may be thought
of as an interval beginning at 7 and extending to inï¬nity to the right (see
Figure 1.4). Of course, inï¬nity is not a number, but we use the symbol
(7, âˆ) to represent all numbers larger than 7. We also use the double
inequality
7 < x < âˆ
to represent this set. Similarly, the symbol (âˆ’âˆ, 12) stands for all num-
bers less than 12. We also use the inequalities âˆ’âˆ< x < 12 to represent
this set.

Deï¬nition
The solution of an equation or inequality in one unknown, say x, is the
collection of all numbers that make the equation or inequality a true
statement. Sometimes this set of numbers is called the solution set. For
example, the inequality
3x âˆ’7 < 8
has as its solution set all numbers less than 5. To demonstrate this we
argue in the following way. If x is a number that satisï¬es the above in-
equality, we can add 7 to both sides and obtain a true statement. That is,
the inequality
3x âˆ’7 + 7 < 8 + 7,
or
3x < 15,
holds. Now, dividing both sides by 3, we obtain x < 5; therefore, if x is
a solution, then it is less than 5. Strictly speaking, however, we have not
proved that every number that is less than 5 is a solution. In an actual
proof, we begin by supposing that x is any number less than 5; that is,
x < 5. We multiply both sides of this inequality by 3 and then subtract 7
from both sides to get
3x âˆ’7 < 8,
the original inequality. Since the hypothesis that x is less than 5 implies
the original inequality, we have proved the result. The important thing
to notice is that the proof consisted in reversing the steps of the original
argument that led to the solution x < 5 in the ï¬rst place. So long as each
step taken is reversible, the above procedure is completely satisfactory
for obtaining solutions of inequalities.
By means of the symbol â‡”, we can give a solution to this example in
compact form. We write
3x âˆ’7 < 8 â‡”3x < 15
(adding 7 to both sides)
and
3x < 15 â‡”x < 5
(dividing both sides by 3).
The solution set is the interval (âˆ’âˆ, 5).
Notation. It is convenient to introduce some terminology and symbols
concerning sets. In general, a set is a collection of objects. The objects
may have any character (number, points, lines, etc.) so long as we know
which objects are in a given set and which are not. If S is a set and P is

an object in it, we write P âˆˆS and say that P is an element of S or that
P belongs to S. If S1 and S2 are two sets, their union, denoted by S1 âˆªS2,
consists of all objects each of which is in at least one of the two sets. The
intersection of S1 and S2, denoted by S1 âˆ©S2, consists of all objects each
of which is in both sets.
Figure 1.5
Schematically, if S1 is the horizontally shaded set of points in Figure
1.5 and S2 is the vertically shaded set, then S1 âˆªS2 consists of the entire
shaded area, and S1 âˆ©S2 consists of the doubly shaded area. Similarly, we
may form the union and intersection of any number of sets. When we
write S1 âˆªS2 âˆª. . . âˆªSn for the union S of the n sets S1, S2, . . . , Sn, then
S consists of all elements each of which is in at least one of the n sets.

brieï¬‚y, n
i1 Si. It may happen that two sets S1 and S2 have no elements
in common. In such a case their intersection is empty, and we use the
term empty set for the set that is devoid of members.
Most often we will deal with sets each of which is speciï¬ed by some
property or properties of its elements. For example, we may speak of the
set of all even integers or the set of all rational numbers between 0 and
1. We employ the special notation
{x : x  2n and n is an integer}
to represent the set of all even integers. In this notation the letter x
stands for a generic element of the set, and the properties that determine
membership in the set are listed to the right of the colon. The notation
{x : x âˆˆ(0, 1) and x is rational}
represents the set of rational numbers in the open interval (0, 1). If a set
has only a few elements, it may be speciï¬ed by listing its members be-
tween braces. Thus the notation {âˆ’2, 0, 1} denotes the set whose elements
are the numbers â€“2, 0, and 1.
The words and and or have precise meanings when used in connection
with sets and their properties. The set consisting of elements that have

1.4
Mathematical Induction
The principle of mathematical induction with which most readers are
familiar can be derived as a consequence of the axioms in Section 1.1.
Since the deï¬nition of natural number is at the basis of the principle of
mathematical induction, we shall develop both concepts together.
Deï¬nition
A set S of numbers is said to be inductive if
(a) 1 âˆˆS and
(b) (x + 1) âˆˆS whenever x âˆˆS.
Examples of inductive sets are easily found. The set of all real numbers
is inductive, as is the set of all rationals. The set of all integers, positive,
zero, and negative, is inductive. The collection of real numbers between
0 and 10 is not inductive, since it satisï¬es (a) but not (b). No ï¬nite set of
real numbers can be inductive since (b) will be violated at some stage.
Deï¬nition
A real number n is said to be a natural number if it belongs to every
inductive set of real numbers. The set of all natural numbers will be
denoted by the symbol N.
We observe that N contains the number 1, since by the deï¬nition of
inductive set, 1 must always be a member of every inductive set. As we
know, the set of natural numbers N is identical with the set of positive integers.
Theorem 1.9
The set N of all natural numbers is an inductive set.

20
1.
The Real Number System
Proof
We must show that N has properties (a) and (b) in the deï¬nition of in-
ductive set. As we remarked above, (a) holds. Now suppose that k is an
element of N. Then k belongs to every inductive set S. For each inductive
set, if k is an element, so is k + 1. Thus k + 1 belongs to every induc-
tive set. Therefore k + 1 belongs to N. Hence N has Property (b), and is
inductive.
The principle of mathematical induction is contained in the next theo-
rem, which asserts that any inductive set of natural numbers must consist
of the entire collection N.
Theorem 1.10 (Principle of mathematical induction)
If S is an inductive set of natural numbers, then S  N.
Proof
Since S is an inductive set, we know from the deï¬nition of natural number
that N is contained in S. On the other hand, since S consists of natural
numbers, it follows that S is contained in N. Therefore S  N.
We now illustrate how the principle of mathematical induction is ap-
plied in practice. The reader may not recognize Theorem 1.10 as the
statement of the familiar principle of mathematical induction. So we shall
prove a formula using Theorem 1.10.
Example 1
Show that
(1.1)
1 + 2 + . . . + n  n(n + 1)
2
for every natural number n.
Solution. Let S be the set of natural numbers n for which the formula (1.1)
holds. We shall show that S is an inductive set.
(a) Clearly 1 âˆˆS, since formula (1.1) holds for n  1.
(b) Suppose k âˆˆS. Then formula (1.1) holds with n  k. Adding (k +1)
to both sides, we see that
1 + . . . + k + (k + 1)  k(k + 1)
2
+ (k + 1)  (k + 1)(k + 2)
2
,
which is formula (1.1) for n  k + 1. Thus (k + 1) is in S whenever
k is.
Combining (a) and (b), we conclude that S is an inductive set of natural
numbers and so consists of all natural numbers. Therefore formula (1.1)
holds for all natural numbers.

Since the set of natural numbers N is identical with the set of positive
integers, the fact that a natural number is positive follows from the axiom
of inequality and the deï¬nition of N.
Theorem 1.12 (The well-ordering principle)
Any nonempty set T of natural numbers contains a smallest element.
Proof
Let k be a member of T. We deï¬ne a set S of natural numbers by the
relation
S  {p : p âˆˆT and p â‰¤k}.
The set S contains a portion (not necessarily all) of the set consisting of
the natural numbers {1, 2, 3, . . . , k âˆ’1, k}. Thus since S is ï¬nite, it has
a smallest element, which we denote by s. We now show that s is the
smallest element of T. First, since s âˆˆS and S âŠ‚T, then s âˆˆT. Suppose
t is any element of T different from s. If t > k, then the inequality k â‰¥s
implies that t > s. On the other hand, if t â‰¤k, then t âˆˆS. Since s is the
smallest element of S and s Ì¸ t, we have s < t. Thus s is smaller than
any other element of T, and the proof is complete.

Continuity and

2.1
Continuity
Most of the functions we study in elementary calculus are described by
formulas. These functions almost always possess derivatives. In fact, a
portion of any ï¬rst course in calculus is devoted to the development of
routine methods for computing derivatives. However, not all functions
possess derivatives everywhere. For example, the functions (1 + x2)/x,
cot x, and sin(1/x) do not possess derivatives at x  0 no matter how
they are deï¬ned at x  0.
As we progress in the study of analysis, it is important to enlarge
substantially the class of functions we examine. Functions that possess
derivatives everywhere form a rather restricted class; extending this
class to functions that are differentiable except at a few isolated points
does not enlarge it greatly. We wish to investigate signiï¬cantly larger
classes of functions, and to do so we introduce the notion of a continuous
function.
Deï¬nitions
Suppose that f is a function from a domain D in R1 to R1. The function f
is continuous at a if (i) the point a is in an open interval I contained in
D, and (ii) for each positive number Îµ there is a positive number Î´ such
that
|f(x) âˆ’f(a)| < Îµ
whenever
|x âˆ’a| < Î´.
23

24
2.

If f is continuous at each point of a set S, we say that f is continuous on
S. A function f is called continuous if it is continuous at every point of
its domain.

The basic theorems of calculus depend for their proofs on certain standard
theorems on limits. These theorems are usually stated without proof in a
ï¬rst course in calculus. In this section we ï¬ll the gap by providing proofs
of the customary theorems on limits. These theorems are the basis for the
formulas for the derivative of the sum, product, and quotient of functions
as well as for the Chain rule.

the result is established.

2.3
One-Sided Limits
The function f : x â†’âˆšx is continuous for all x > 0, and since

in Section 2.1 is not fulï¬lled at x  0. We now extend the deï¬nition of
continuous function so that a function such as âˆšx will have the natural
property of continuity at the endpoint of its domain. For this purpose we
need the concept of a one-sided limit.
Deï¬nition
Suppose that f is a function from a domain D in R1 to R1. The function f
tends to L as x tends to a from the right if (i) there is an open interval I
in D that has a as its left endpoint and (ii) for each Îµ > 0 there is a Î´ > 0
such that
|f(x) âˆ’L| < Îµ
whenever
0 < x âˆ’a < Î´.
If f tends to L as x tends to a from the right, we write
f(x) â†’L
as
x â†’a+,
and we denote the number L by
lim
xâ†’a+ f(x).
A similar deï¬nition is employed for limits from the left. In this case the
condition 0 < x âˆ’a < Î´ is replaced by
0 < a âˆ’x < Î´,
and the notation f(x) â†’L as x â†’aâˆ’,
lim
xâ†’aâˆ’f(x)  L,
is used.
The condition 0 < |x âˆ’a| < Î´ is equivalent to the two conditions
0 < x âˆ’a < Î´ and 0 < a âˆ’x < Î´. All the theorems on limits that were
stated and proved in Section 2.2 used the inequality 0 < |x âˆ’a| < Î´.
As a result, all the theorems on limits imply corresponding theorems for
limits from the left and limits from the right.
The deï¬nition of one-sided limit leads to the deï¬nition of one-sided
continuity.
Deï¬nitions
A function f is continuous on the right at a if a is in the domain of f
and f(x) â†’f(a) as x â†’a+. The function f is continuous on the left at
a if a is in the domain of f and f(x) â†’f(a) as x â†’aâˆ’.

2.
Continuity and Limits
If the domain of a function f is a ï¬nite interval, say a â‰¤x â‰¤b, then
limits and continuity at the endpoints are of the one-sided variety. For
example, the function f : x â†’x2âˆ’3x+5 deï¬ned on the interval 2 â‰¤x â‰¤4
is continuous on the right at x  2 and continuous on the left at x  4.
The following is the general deï¬nition of continuity for functions from a
set in R1 to R1.
Deï¬nitions
Let f be a function from a domain in R1 to R1. The function f is contin-

is a Î´ > 0 such that
(2.3)
|f(x) âˆ’f(a)| < Îµ
whenever x âˆˆD and
|x âˆ’a| < Î´.
A function f is continuous on D if it is continuous with respect to D
at every point of D.
The phrase â€œwith respect to Dâ€ is usually omitted in the deï¬nition of
continuity since the context will always make the situation clear.
If a is a point of an open interval I contained in D, then the above
deï¬nition of continuity coincides with that given in Section 2.1.
Deï¬nition
A point a is an isolated point of a set D in R1 if there is an open interval
I such that the set I âˆ©D consists of the single point {a}.
A function f from a domain D in R1 to R1 is continuous at every isolated
point of D. To see this, let a be an isolated point of D and suppose I is such
that {a}  I âˆ©D. We choose Î´ > 0 so small that the condition |x âˆ’a| < Î´
implies that x is in I. Then condition (ii) in the deï¬nition of continuity is
always satisï¬ed, since (ii) holds when x  a, the only point in question.
The theorems of Section 2.2 carry over almost without change to the
case of one-sided limits. As an illustration we state a one-sided version of
Theorem 2.5, the limit of a composite function.
Theorem 2.9

The proof of Theorem 2.9 follows precisely the line of proof of Theorem
2.5. Although we always require x > a when g(x) â†’L, it is not true that
g(x) remains always larger than or always smaller than L. Hence it is
necessary to assume that f is continuous at L and not merely continuous
on one side.

An inï¬nite sequence may or may not tend to a limit. When an inï¬nite
sequence does tend to a limit, the rules of operation are similar to those
given in Section 2.2 for limits of functions.
Deï¬nition

is a natural number N such that |xn âˆ’L| < Îµ for all n > N. We also write
xn â†’L as n â†’âˆ, and we say that the sequence {xn} has L as a limit.âˆ—

without bound, we write xn â†’âˆ’âˆas n â†’âˆ.
The theorems on limit of a constant, limit of equal functions, limit of
a product, limit of a quotient, limit of inequalities, and the Sandwiching
theorem have corresponding statements for sequences.

to the next result, which we state without proof.
Theorem 2.13

an integer N such that f (xn) is deï¬ned for all integers n > N; furthermore,

The next axiom, when added to those of Chapter 1, serves to describe
the real number system uniquely.
Axiom C (Axiom of continuity)
Suppose that an inï¬nite sequence x1,x2, . . . ,xn, . . . is such that xn+1 â‰¥xn for
all n, and there is a number M such that xn â‰¤M for all n. Then there is a
number L â‰¤M such that xn â†’L as n â†’âˆand xn â‰¤L for all n.
The situation is shown in Figure 2.6. If we plot the numbers xn on a
horizontal line, the corresponding points move steadily to the right as n
increases. It is geometrically evident that they must cluster at some value
such as L that cannot exceed the number M.
Figure 2.6 xn tends to L as n â†’âˆ.
By means of Axiom C we establish the following simple result.
Theorem 2.14
(a) There is no real number M that exceeds all positive integers.
(b) If xn â‰¥n for every positive integer n, then xn â†’+âˆas n â†’âˆ.
(c) limnâ†’âˆ(1/n)  0.
Proof
(a) Let xn  n and suppose there is a number M such that M â‰¥xn for
every n. By Axiom C there is a number L such that xn â†’L as n â†’âˆ
and xn â‰¤L for all n. Choose Îµ  1 in the deï¬nition of limit. Then for
a sufï¬ciently large integer N, it follows that L âˆ’1 < xn < L + 1 for
all n > N. But xN+1  N + 1, and so L âˆ’1 < N + 1 < L + 1. Hence
N + 2  xN+2 > L, contrary to the statement xn â‰¤L for all n.

(c) Let Îµ > 0 be given. There is an integer N such that N > 1/Îµ. Then if
n > N, clearly 1/n < Îµ. The result follows.

3.1
The Intermediate-Value Theorem
The proofs of many theorems of calculus require a knowledge of the
basic properties of continuous functions. In this section we establish the
Intermediate-value theorem, an essential tool used in the proofs of the
Fundamental theorem of calculus and the Mean-value theorem.
Theorem 3.1 (Nested intervals theorem)
Suppose that
In  {x : an â‰¤x â‰¤bn},
n  1, 2, . . . ,
is a sequence of closed intervals such that In+1 âŠ‚In for each n. If limnâ†’âˆ(bnâˆ’

Proof
By hypothesis, we have an â‰¤an+1 and bn â‰¤bn+1. Since an < bn for
every n, the sequence {an} is nondecreasing and bounded from above
by b1 (see Figure 3.1). Similarly, the sequence bn is nonincreasing and
bounded from below by a1. Using Axiom C, we conclude that there are
numbers x0 and xâ€²
0 such that an â†’x0, an â‰¤x0, and bn â†’xâ€²
0, bn â‰¥xâ€²
0, as
n â†’âˆ. Using the fact that bn âˆ’an â†’0, n â†’âˆ, we ï¬nd that x0  xâ€²
0
and
an â‰¤x0 â‰¤bn
for every n. Thus x0 is in every In.

Before proving the main theorem, we prove the following result, which
we shall use often in this chapter.
Theorem 3.2
Suppose f is continuous on [a,b], xn âˆˆ[a,b] for each n, and xn â†’x0. Then
x0 âˆˆ[a,b] and f (xn) â†’f (x0).
Proof
Since xn â‰¥a for each n, it follows from the theorem on the limit of
inequalities for sequences that x0 â‰¥a. Similarly, x0 â‰¤b so x0 âˆˆ[a, b].
If a < x0 < b, then f(xn) â†’f(x0) on account of the composite function
theorem (Theorem 2.5). The same conclusion holds if x0  a or b. A
detailed proof of the last sentence is left to the reader.
We now establish the main theorem of this section.
Theorem 3.3 (Intermediate-value theorem)
Suppose f is continuous on [a,b], c âˆˆR1, f (a) < c, and f (b) > c. Then there
is at least one number x0 on [a,b] such that f (x0)  c.
Note that there may be more than one such x0, as indicated in Figure
3.2.
Proof
Deï¬ne a1  a and b1  b. Then observe that f((a1 + b1)/2) is either equal
to c, greater than c, or less than c. If it equals c, choose x0  (a1 + b1)/2
and the result is proved. If f((a1 + b1)/2) > c, then deï¬ne a2  a1 and
b2  (a1 + b1)/2. If f((a1 + b1)/2) < c, then deï¬ne a2  ((a1 + b1)/2)
and b2  b1.
In each of the last two cases we have f(a2) < c and f(b2) > c. Again
compute f((a2 + b2)/2). If this value equals c, the result is proved. If

In this section we prove an important principle about real numbers that
is often used as an axiom in place of Axiom C.
Deï¬nitions
A set S of real numbers has the upper bound M if x â‰¤M for every
number x in S; we also say that the set S is bounded above by M. The set
S has the lower bound m if x â‰¥m for every number x in S; we also
say that S is bounded below by m. A set S is bounded if S has an upper
and a lower bound. Suppose that f is a function on R1 whose domain D
contains the set S. We denote by f |S the restriction of f to the set S;
that is, f |S has domain S and f |S(x)  f(x) for all x in S. A function f is
bounded above, bounded below, or bounded if the set R consisting of the
range of f satisï¬es the corresponding condition.

Corollary

With the help of this corollary it is possible to show that the Axiom of

Deï¬nitions
Let f have an interval I of R1 as its domain and a set in R1 as its range.
We say that f is increasing on I if f(x2) > f(x1) whenever x2 > x1. The
function f is nondecreasing on I if f(x2) â‰¥f(x1) whenever x2 > x1. The
function f is decreasing on I if f(x2) < f(x1) whenever x2 > x1. The
function f is nonincreasing on I if f(x2) â‰¤f(x1) whenever x2 > x1. A

If a function f deï¬ned on an interval I is continuous on I, then an
extension of the Intermediate-value theorem shows that the range of
f is an interval. In order to establish this result, we use the following
characterization of an interval on R1.
Theorem 3.5
A set S in R1is an interval â‡”(i) S contains more than one point and (ii) for
every x1,x2 âˆˆS the number x is in S whenever x âˆˆ(x1,x2).
Proof
If S is an interval, then clearly the two properties hold. Therefore, we
suppose that (i) and (ii) hold and show that S is an interval. We consider
two cases.
Case 1: S is bounded. Deï¬ne a  inf S and b  sup S. Let x be an
element of the open interval (a, b). We shall show that x âˆˆS.

Since a  inf S, we use the corollary to Theorem 3.4 to assert that
there is an x1 âˆˆS with x1 < x. Also, since b  sup S, there is an x2 in S
with x2 > x. Hence x âˆˆ(x1, x2), which by (ii) of the hypothesis shows that
x âˆˆS. We conclude that every element of (a, b) is in S. Thus S is either
a closed interval, an open interval, or a half-open interval; its endpoints
are a, b.
Case 2: S is unbounded in one direction. Assume that S is unbounded
above and bounded below, the case of S bounded above and unbounded
below being similar. Let a  inf S. Then S âŠ‚[a, âˆ). Let x be any number
such that x > a. We shall show that x âˆˆS. As in Case 1, there is a number
x1 âˆˆS such that x1 < x. Since S has no upper bound there is an x2 âˆˆS
such that x2 > x. Using (ii) of the hypothesis, we conclude that x âˆˆS.
Therefore, S  [a, âˆ) or S  (a, âˆ).
We now establish a stronger form of the Intermediate-value theorem.
Theorem 3.6
Suppose that the domain of f is an interval I and f is continuous on I. Assume
that f is not constant on I. Then the range of f , denoted by J, is an interval.
Proof
We shall show that J has properties (i) and (ii) of Theorem 3.5 and there-
fore is an interval. Since f is not constant, its range must have more than
one point, and property (i) is established. Now let y1, y2 âˆˆJ. Then there
are numbers x1, x2 âˆˆI such that f(x1)  y1 and f(x2)  y2. We may as-
sume that x1 < x2. The function f is continuous on [x1, x2], and so we
may apply the Intermediate-value theorem. If c is any number between
y1 and y2, there is an x0 âˆˆ[x1, x2] such that f(x0)  c. Thus c âˆˆJ, and we
have established property (ii). The set J is an interval.
If f is continuous on I  {x : a â‰¤x â‰¤b}, it is not necessarily the case
that J is determined by f(a) and f(b). Figure 3.6 shows that J may exceed
the interval [f(a), f(b)]. In fact, I may be bounded and J unbounded, as
is illustrated by the function f : x â†’1/x with I  {x : 0 < x < 1}.
The range is J  {y : 1 < y < âˆ}. The function f : x â†’1/(1 + x2)
with I  {x : 0 < x < âˆ} and J  {y : 0 < y < 1} is an example of a
continuous function with an unbounded domain and a bounded range.
Consider the restriction to [0, âˆ) of the function f : x â†’xn where n is a
positive integer. By Theorem 3.6, the range of f is an interval, which must
be [0, âˆ), since f(0)  0, f is increasing, and f(x) â†’+âˆas x â†’+âˆ.

Theorem 3.7 (Bolzanoâ€“Weierstrass Theorem)
Any bounded inï¬nite sequence of real numbers contains a convergent
subsequence.

3.4
The Boundedness and
Extreme-Value Theorems
In this section we establish additional basic properties of continuous
functions from R1 to R1. The Boundedness and Extreme-value theorems
proved below are essential in the proofs of the basic theorems in differ-
ential calculus. The Boundedness theorem shows that a function that is
continuous on a closed interval must have a bounded range. The Extreme-
value theorem adds additional precise information about such functions.
It states that the supremum and the inï¬mum of the values in the range
are also in the range.
Theorem 3.8 (Boundedness Theorem)
Suppose that the domain of f is the closed interval I  {x:a â‰¤x â‰¤b}, and f
is continuous on I. Then the range of f is bounded.
Proof
We shall assume that the range is unbounded and reach a contradiction.
Suppose that for each positive integer n, there is an xn âˆˆI such that
|f(xn)| > n. The sequence {xn} is bounded, and by the Bolzanoâ€“Weierstrass
theorem, there is a convergent subsequence y1, y2, . . . , yn, . . ., where
yn  xkn, that converges to an element x0 âˆˆI. Since f is continuous
on I, we have f(yn) â†’f(x0) as n â†’âˆ. Choosing Îµ  1, we know that
there is an N1 such that for n > N1, we have
|f(yn) âˆ’f(x0)| < 1
whenever
n > N1.
For these n it follows that
|f(yn)| < |f(x0)| + 1
for all
n > N1.
On the other hand,
|f(yn)|  |f(xkn)| > kn â‰¥n
for all
n.
Combining these results, we obtain
n < |f(x0)| + 1
for all
n > N1.
Clearly, we may choose n larger than |f(x0)| + 1, which is a contradic-
tion.

3.5

In the deï¬nition of continuity of a function f at a point x0, it is necessary to

since F covers I, there is a member of F, say J  {x : Î± < x < Î²}, such
that x0 âˆˆJ. By choosing N sufï¬ciently large, we can ï¬nd an IN contained
in J. But this contradicts the fact that inï¬nitely many intervals of F are
required to cover IN, since we found that the one interval J covers IN.
In the Heineâ€“Borel theorem, the hypothesis that I  {x : a â‰¤x â‰¤b}
is a closed interval is crucial. The open interval J1  {x : 0 < x < 1} is
covered by the family F of intervals (3.9), but no ï¬nite subfamily of F
covers J1.

4.1
The Derivative in R1
In a ï¬rst course in calculus we learn a number of theorems on differenti-
ation, but usually the proofs are not provided. In this section we review
those elementary theorems and provide the missing proofs for the basic
results.

f â€² is in R1.

The Extreme-value theorem (Theorem 3.9) shows that a function
which is continuous on a closed interval I takes on its maximum and
minimum values there. If the maximum value occurs at an interior point
of I, and if the function possesses a derivative at that point, the following
result gives a method for locating maximum (and minimum) values of
such functions.
Theorem 4.10
Suppose that f is continuous on an interval I and that f takes on its maximum
value at x0, an interior point of I. If f â€²(x0) exists, then
f â€²(x0)  0.
The proof follows immediately from the deï¬nition of derivative. A
similar result holds for functions that take on their minimum value at an
interior point. See Figure 4.1.
The next two theorems, Rolleâ€™s theorem and the Mean-value theo-
rem, form the groundwork for further developments in the theory of
differentiation.

then there is a number x0 âˆˆI1 such that f â€²(x0)  0.

4.2
Inverse Functions in R1
We have seen in Section 1.2 that a relation from R1 to R1 is a set of
ordered pairs of real numbers; that is, a relation is a set in R2. A function
from R1 to R1 is a particular case of a relation, one in which no two ordered
pairs have the same ï¬rst element.
Deï¬nition
Suppose that S is a relation from R1 to R1. We deï¬ne the inverse relation
of S as the set of pairs (x, y) such that (y, x) is in S.
If S is the solution set in R2 of an equation such as f(x, y)  0, the
inverse of S is the solution set in R2 of the equation f(y, x)  0. Suppose
that f is a function, and we consider the ordered pairs in R2 that form
the graph of the equation y  f(x). Then the inverse of f is the graph
of the equation x  f(y). The simplest examples of functions show that
the inverse of a function is a relation, but not necessarily a function. For
example, the function f : x â†’x2, with graph y  x2, has for its inverse
the relation whose graph is given by y2  x. This inverse relation is not
a function.
Theorem 4.17 (Inverse function theorem)
Suppose that f is a continuous, increasing function that has an interval I for
domain and has range J. (See Figure 4.3.) Then,
(a) J is an interval.

continuous and increasing on J.
(c) We have

A similar result holds if f is decreasing on I.

5.2
The Riemann Integral
In addition to the development of the integral by the method of Darboux,
there is a technique due to Riemann that starts with a direct approxima-
tion of the integral by a sum. The main result of this section shows that
the two deï¬nitions of integral are equivalent.

We omit the proof of the theorem that shows that every Darboux
integrable function is Riemann integrable. Thus we state the following
result:
Theorem 5.10
A function f is Riemann integrable on [a,b] if and only if it is Darboux
integrable on [a,b].
In light of Theorem 5.10 we shall now drop the terms Darboux and
Riemann, and just refer to functions as integrable.

6.1
The Schwarz and Triangle
Inequalities; Metric Spaces
In Chapters 2 through 5 we developed many properties of functions from
R1 into R1 with the purpose of proving the basic theorems in differen-
tial and integral calculus of one variable. The next step in analysis is the
establishment of the basic facts needed in proving the theorems of cal-
culus in two and more variables. One way would be to prove extensions
of the theorems of Chapters 2â€“5 for functions from R2 into R1, then for
functions from R3 into R1, and so forth. However, all these results can
be encompassed in one general theory obtained by introducing the con-
cept of a metric space and by considering functions with domain in one
metric space and with range in a second metric space. In this chapter we
introduce the fundamentals of this theory, and in the following chapters
we apply the results to differentiation and integration in Euclidean space
in any number of dimensions.

In this chapter we shall be concerned with sets or collections of ele-
ments, which may be chosen in any manner whatsoever. A set will be
considered fully described whenever we can determine whether or not
any given element is a member of the set.
Deï¬nition

Deï¬nition
Let S be a set and suppose d is a function with domain consisting of all
pairs of points of S and with range in R1. That is, d is a function from S Ã— S
into R1. We say that S and the function d form a metric space when the
function d satisï¬es the following conditions
(i) d(x, y) â‰¥0 for all (x, y) âˆˆS Ã— S; and d(x, y)  0 if and only if x  y.
(ii) d(y, x)  d(x, y) for all (x, y) âˆˆS Ã— S.
(iii) d(x, z) â‰¤d(x, y) + d(y, z) for all (x, y, z) âˆˆS. (Triangle inequality.)
The function d satisfying conditions (i), (ii), and (iii) is called the met-
ric, or distance, function in S. Hence a metric space consists of the pair
(S, d).

6.2
Topology in Metric Spaces
We now develop some of the basic properties of metric spaces. From an
intuitive viewpoint we frequently think of a metric space as Euclidean
space of one, two, or three dimensions. However, the deï¬nitions and
theorems apply to all metric spaces, many of which are far removed from

6.2.
Topology in Metric Spaces
107
ordinary Euclidean space. For this reason we designate the properties as
the topology of the space.
For convenience we will use the letter S to denote a metric space with
the understanding that a metric d is attached to S.
Deï¬nitions
Let p1, p2, . . . , pn, . . . denote a sequence of elements of a metric space S.
We use the symbol {pn} to denote such a sequence. Suppose p0 âˆˆS. We
say that pn tends to p0 as n tends to inï¬nity if d(pn, p0) â†’0 as n â†’âˆ.
The notations pn â†’p0 and limnâ†’âˆpn  p0 will be used.
Let p0 be an element of S, a metric space, and suppose r is a positive
number. The open ball with center at p0 and radius r is the set B(p0, r)
given by
B(p0, r)  {p âˆˆS : d(p, p0) < r}.
The closed ball with center at p0 and radius r is the set B(p0, r) given
by
B(p0, r)  {p âˆˆS : d(p, p0) â‰¤r}.

Deï¬nition
Let A be a set in a metric space S and suppose p0 is an element of A. We
say that p0 is an isolated point of A if for some positive number r there
is an open ball B(p0, r) such that
B(p0, r) âˆ©A  {p0},
where {p0} is the set consisting of the single element p0.
For example, in R1 with the Euclidean metric the set A  {x : 1 â‰¤x â‰¤
2, x  3, 4} has the elements 3 and 4 as isolated points of A. None of the
points in the interval 1 â‰¤x â‰¤2 is an isolated point of A.
Deï¬nition
A point p0 is a limit point of a set A if every open ball B(p0, r) contains
a point p of A that is distinct from p0. Note that p0 may or may not be an
element of A.
For example, in R1 with the usual Euclidean metric deï¬ned by
d(x1, x2)  |x1 âˆ’x2|, the set C  {x : 1 â‰¤x < 3} has x  3 as a
limit point. In fact, every member of C is a limit point of C.
A set A in a metric space is closed if A contains all of its limit points.
A set A in a metric space S is open if each point p0 in A is the center
of an open ball B(p0, r) that is contained in A. That is, B(p0, r) âŠ‚A. It is
important to notice that the radius r may change from point to point in
A.
Theorem 6.3
A point p0 is a limit point of a set A if and only if there is a sequence {pn} with
pn âˆˆA and pn Ì¸ p0 for every n and such that pn â†’p0 as n â†’âˆ.

Proof

with p0 as center will have points of the sequence (all Ì¸ p0). Thus p0 is a
limit point.
(b) Suppose p0 is a limit point of A. We construct a sequence {pn} with the
desired properties. According to the deï¬nition of limit point, the open ball
B(p0, 1
2 ) has a point of A. Let p1 be such a point. Deï¬ne r2  d(p0, p1)/2
and construct the ball B(p0, r2). There are points of A in this ball (different
from p0), and we denote one of them by p2. Next, deï¬ne r3  d(p0, p2)/2
and choose p3 âˆˆA in the ball B(p0, r3). Continuing this process, we see
that rn < 1/2n â†’0 as n â†’âˆ. Since d(p0, pn) < rn, it follows that {pn}
is a sequence tending to p0, and the proof is complete.
Theorem 6.4
(a) An open ball is an open set.
(b) A closed ball is a closed set.

Let S be a space of elements, by which we mean that S is the totality of
points (or elements) under consideration. A set of points is a collection
of elements in S. We shall often deal with a set of sets, that is, a set whose
elements are sets in S. We speak of such a set as a family of sets and
denote it by a script letter such as F. If the number of sets in the family is

the members. For example, {A1, A2, . . . , An}, where each Ai is a set in S,
is a ï¬nite family of sets.

as the set S âˆ’A.

Theorem 6.6
(a) Let A be any set in a metric space S. Then A is closed â‡”C(A) is open.
(b) The space S is both open and closed.
(c) The null set is both open and closed.
Proof
We ï¬rst assume that A is closed and prove that C(A) is open. Let p âˆˆC(A)
and suppose that there is no open ball about p lying entirely in C(A). We
shall reach a contradiction. If there is no such ball, then every ball about
p contains points of A (necessarily different from p, since p âˆˆC(A)). This
fact asserts that p is a limit point of A. But since A is assumed closed, we
conclude that p is in A, which contradicts the fact that p âˆˆC(A). Thus
there is an open ball about p in C(A), and so C(A) is open.
The proof that A is closed if C(A) is open is similar.
Part (b) is a direct consequence of the deï¬nitions of open and closed
sets. Part (c) follows immediately from parts (a) and (b), since the null
set is the complement of S.
Theorem 6.7
(a) The intersection of any family F of closed sets is closed.
(b) Let A1,A2, . . . ,An be a ï¬nite family of closed sets. Then the union n
i1 Ai
is closed.
The proof of Theorem 6.7 is an immediate consequence of the
deï¬nition of a closed set and the deï¬nition of the intersection of sets.
It is important to observe that Part (b) of Theorem 6.7 is false if the
word ï¬nite is dropped from the hypothesis. It is not difï¬cult to construct

6.

an inï¬nite family of closed sets such that the union of the family is not a
closed set.
Deï¬nition
Let {pn} be an inï¬nite sequence of points. An inï¬nite sequence of points
{qn} is called a subsequence of {pn} if there is an increasing sequence of
positive integers k1, k2, . . . , kn, . . . such that for every n,
qn  pkn.
To illustrate subsequences, observe that the sequence
p1, p2, . . . , pn, . . .
has subsequences such as
p1, p3, p5, . . . ,
p5, p10, p15, . . . ,
p1, p4, p7, . . . .
It is clear that the subscripts {kn} of a subsequence have the property that
kn â‰¥n for every n. This fact may be proved by induction.
Theorem 6.8
Suppose that {pn} is an inï¬nite sequence in a metric space S, and that {qn} is
a subsequence. If pn â†’p0 as n â†’âˆ, then qn â†’p0 as n â†’âˆ.
(Every subsequence of a convergent sequence converges to the same limit.)
Proof
Let Îµ > 0 be given. From the deï¬nition of convergence, there is a positive
integer N such that d(pn, p0) < Îµ for all n > N. However, for each n, we

6.3
Countable and Uncountable Sets
If A and B are sets with a ï¬nite number of elements, we can easily com-
pare their sizes by pairing the elements of A with those of B. If after we
complete the pairing process there remain unpaired members of one of
the sets, say of A, then A is said to be larger than B. The same situation
holds for sets with inï¬nitely many elements. While many pairs of inï¬nite
sets can be matched in a one-to-one way, it is a remarkable fact that there
are inï¬nite sets that cannot be paired with each other. The problem of
the relative sizes of sets with inï¬nitely many members has been studied
extensively, and in this section we shall describe only the basic proper-
ties of the sizes of ï¬nite and inï¬nite sets. These properties and the more
sophisticated developments of the theory of inï¬nite sets turn out to be
useful tools in the development of analysis. The theorems we consider
below pertain to sets in RN.
Deï¬nitions
A set S is denumerable if S can be put into one-to-one correspondence
with the positive integers. A set S is countable if S is either ï¬nite or
denumerable.

6.3.
Countable and Uncountable Sets
115
Theorem 6.9
(a) Any nonempty subset of the natural numbers is countable.
(b) Any subset of a countable set is countable.
Proof
To establish Part (a), let S be the given nonempty subset. Since S is a subset
of the positive integers, it must have a smallest element. We denote it by
k1. If S  {k1}, then S has exactly one element. If the set S âˆ’{k1} is not
empty, it has a smallest element, which we denote by k2. We continue the
process. If for some integer n, the set S âˆ’{k1, k2, . . . , kn} is empty, then
S is ï¬nite and we are done. Otherwise, the sequence k1, k2, . . . , kn, . . .
is inï¬nite. We shall show that S  {k1, k2, . . . , kn, . . .}. Suppose, on the
contrary, that there is an element of S not among the kn. Then there is
a smallest such element, which we denote by p. Now, k1 is the smallest
element of S, and so k1 â‰¤p. Let T be those elements of k1, k2, . . . , kn, . . .
with the property that ki â‰¤p. Then T is a ï¬nite set, and there is an
integer i such that ki â‰¤p < ki+1 for some i. Since p is not equal to any
of the kj, it follows that ki < p < ki+1. By construction, the element
ki+1 is the smallest element of S that is larger than k1, k2, . . . , kj. Since p
is in S, we have a contradiction of the inequality ki < p < ki+1. Thus
S  {k1, k2, . . . , kn, . . .}.
Part (b) is a direct consequence of part (a).
Theorem 6.10
The union of a countable family of countable sets is countable.
Proof
Let S1, S2, . . . , Sm, . . . be a countable family of countable sets. For each

or all of the pairs (m, 1), (m, 2), . . . , (m, n), . . .. Hence the union S can be
put into one-to-one correspondence with a subset of the totality of ordered
pairs (m, n) with m, n  1, 2, . . .. However, the totality of ordered pairs
can be arranged in a single sequence. We ï¬rst write the ordered pairs as
shown in Figure 6.4.

An open cell is RN Is a set of the form

Theorem 6.12
An open set in R1 is the union of a countable family of disjoint open intervals.
Proof
Let G be the given open set and suppose x0 âˆˆG. Since G is open, there
is an open interval J that contains x0 and such that J âŠ‚G. Deï¬ne I(x0) as
the union of all such open intervals J. Then I(x0) is open and contained
in G. Furthermore, since the union of a set of open intervals having a
common point is an open interval, I(x0) is an open interval. Let x1 and x2
be distinct points of G. We shall show that I(x1) and I(x2) are disjoint or that
I(x1) â‰¡I(x2). Suppose that I(x1)âˆ©I(x2) is not empty. Then I(x1)âˆªI(x2) is an
interval in G that contains both x1 and x2. Hence I(x1) âˆªI(x2) is contained
in both I(x1) and I(x2). Therefore, I(x1) â‰¡I(x2). Thus G is composed of the
union of disjoint open intervals of the type I(x0), and by Theorem 6.10
the union is countable.

6.4
Compact Sets and the Heineâ€“Borel
Theorem
In Theorem 3.7 we established the Bolzanoâ€“Weierstrass theorem, which
states that any bounded inï¬nite sequence in R1 contains a convergent sub-
sequence. In this section we investigate the possibility of extending this
theorem to metric spaces. We shall give an example below which shows
that the theorem does not hold if the bounded sequence in R1 is replaced
by a bounded sequence in a metric space S. However, the important no-
tion of compactness, which we now deï¬ne, leads to a modiï¬ed extension
of the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem.
Deï¬nition
A set A in a metric space S is compact if each sequence of points {pn} in
A contains a subsequence, say {qn}, that converges to a point p0 in A.
We see immediately from the deï¬nition of compactness and from the
Bolzanoâ€“Weierstrass theorem that every closed, bounded set in R1 is com-
pact. Also, it is important to notice that the subsequence {qn} not only
converges to p0 but that p0 is in A.
Deï¬nition
A set A in a metric space S is bounded if A is contained in some ball
B(p, r) with r > 0.
Theorem 6.13
A compact set A in a metric space S is bounded and closed.
Proof
Assume that A is compact and not bounded. We shall reach a contradic-
tion. Deï¬ne the sequence {pn} as follows: Choose p1 âˆˆA. Since A is not
bounded, there is an element p2 in A such that d(p1, p2) > 1. Continuing,
for each n, there is an element pn in A with d(p1, pn) > n âˆ’1. Since A is

6.4.
Compact Sets and the Heineâ€“Borel Theorem
119
compact, we can ï¬nd a subsequence {qn} of {pn} such that qn converges
to an element p0 âˆˆA. From the deï¬nition of convergence, there is an
integer N such that d(qn, p0) < 1 for all n â‰¥N. But
(6.5)
d(qn, p1) â‰¤d(qn, p0) + d(p0, p1) < 1 + d(p0, p1).
Since qn is actually at least the n th member of the sequence {pn}, it follows
that d(qn, p1) > n âˆ’1. For n sufï¬ciently large, this inequality contradicts
inequality (6.5). Hence A is bounded.
Now assume that A is not closed. Then there is a limit point p0 of
A that is not in A. According to Theorem 6.4, there is a sequence {pn}
of elements in A such that pn â†’p0 as n â†’âˆ. But any subsequence
of a convergent sequence converges to the same element. Hence the
deï¬nition of compactness is contradicted unless p0 âˆˆA.
It is natural to ask whether or not the converse of Theorem 6.13 holds.
It is not difï¬cult to give an example of a sequence of bounded elements
in a metric space that has no convergent subsequence. Thus any set
containing such a sequence, even if it is bounded and closed, cannot
be compact.
For some metric spaces the property of being bounded and closed is
equivalent to compactness. The next theorem establishes this equiva-
lence for the spaces RN with a Euclidean or equivalent metric.
Theorem 6.14
A bounded, closed set in RN is compact.
Proof
Let A be bounded and closed. Then A lies in a cell

Deï¬nition
A sequence {pn} in a metric space S is a Cauchy sequence if for every
Îµ > 0 there is a positive integer N such that d(pn, pm) < Îµ whenever
n, m > N.
We know that a Cauchy sequence in R1 converges to a limit (Section

limit. Writing pn  (p1

the sequence {pk}. However, we cannot conclude that in a general metric
space a Cauchy sequence always converges to an element in the space.
In fact, the statement is false. To see this, consider the metric space (S, d)
consisting of all rational numbers with the metric d(a, b)  |a âˆ’b|. Since
a Cauchy sequence of rational numbers may converge to an irrational
number, it follows that a Cauchy sequence in S may not converge to a
limit in S.
Let H be a set in a space S and suppose that F is a family of sets in
S. The family F covers H if every point of H is a point in at least one
member of F. Coverings of compact sets by a ï¬nite number of open balls
have a natural extension to coverings by any family of open sets. The
next theorem, an important and useful one in analysis, gives one form of
this result. It is a direct extension of Theorem 3.12.

6.5
Functions on Compact Sets
In Chapter 3, we discussed the elementary properties of functions de-
ï¬ned on R1 with range in R1. We were also concerned with functions
whose domain consisted of a part of R1, usually an interval. In this sec-
tion we develop properties of functions with domain all or part of an
arbitrary metric space S and with range in R1. That is, we consider real-
valued functions. We ï¬rst take up the fundamental notions of limits and
continuity.
Deï¬nition
Suppose that f is a function with domain A, a subset of a metric space S,
and with range in R1; we write f : A â†’R1. We say that f(p) tends to l

Observe that the above deï¬nition does not require f(p0) to be deï¬ned,

Deï¬nitions
Let A be a subset of a metric space S, and suppose f : A â†’R1 is given.
Let p0 âˆˆA. We say that f is continuous with respect to A at p0 if (i)
f(p0) is deï¬ned, and (ii) either p0 is an isolated point of A or p0 is a limit
point of A and
f(p) â†’f(p0) as p â†’p0,
p âˆˆA.
We say that f is continuous on A if f is continuous with respect to A at
every point of A.
If the domain of f is the entire metric space S, then we say that f is
continuous at p0, omitting the phrase â€œwith respect to A.â€ The deï¬nitions
of limit and continuity for functions with domain in one metric space S1
and range in another metric space S2 are extensions of those given above.
The basic properties of functions deï¬ned on R1 discussed in Chapter
3 do not always have direct analogues for functions deï¬ned on a subset
of a metric space. For example, the Intermediate-value theorem (Theo-
rem 3.3) for functions deï¬ned on an interval of R1 does not carry over to
real-valued functions deï¬ned on all or part of a metric space. However,
when the domain of a real-valued function is a compact subset of a met-
ric space, many of the theorems of Chapter 3 have natural extensions.
For example, the following theorem is the analogue of the boundedness
theorem (Theorem 3.8).
Theorem 6.16
Let A be a compact subset of a metric space S. Suppose that f :A â†’R1 is
continuous on A. Then the range of f is bounded.
Proof
We assume that the range is unbounded and reach a contradiction.
Suppose that for each positive integer n, there is a pn âˆˆA such that
|f(pn)| > n. Since A is compact, the sequence {pn} âŠ‚A must have a
convergent subsequence, say {qn}, and qn â†’Â¯p with Â¯p âˆˆA. Since f is
continuous on A, we have f(qn) â†’f(Â¯p) as n â†’âˆ. Choosing Îµ  1 in the
deï¬nition of continuity on A and observing that d(qn, Â¯p) â†’0 as n â†’âˆ,
we can state that there is an N1 such that for n > N1, we have
|f(qn) âˆ’f(Â¯p)| < 1
whenever
n > N1.

6.
Elementary Theory of Metric Spaces
We choose N1 so large that |f(Â¯p)| < N1. Now for n > N1 we may write
|f(qn)|  |f(qn) âˆ’f(Â¯p) + f(Â¯p)| â‰¤|f(qn) âˆ’f(Â¯p)| + |f(Â¯p)| < 1 + |f(Â¯p)|.
Since qn is at least the nth member of the sequence {pn}, it follows that
|f(qn)| > n for each n. Therefore,
n < |f(qn)| < 1 + |f(Â¯p)| < 1 + N1,
a contradiction for n sufï¬ciently large. Hence the range of f
is
bounded.
Note the similarity of the above proof to that of Theorem 3.8. Also,
observe the essential manner in which the compactness of A is employed.
The result clearly does not hold if A is not compact.

generalization of ordinary differentiation discussed in Chapter 4 to partial
differentation of functions from RN to R1. In Section 7.3, we extend the
ordinary derivative to the total derivative.

Deï¬nitions
Let f be a function from RN into R1. We deï¬ne the second partial deriva-

In computing second partial derivatives it is natural to ask whether
the order of computation affects the result. That is, is it always true that
f,i,j  f,j,i for i Ì¸ j? There are simple examples that show that the order of
computation may lead to different results. (See Problem 3 at the end of
this section.) The next theorem, stated without proof, gives a sufï¬cient
condition that validates the interchange of order of partial differentiation.

For functions from R1 into R1 the second derivative test is one of the
most useful for identifying the maximum and minimum points on the
graph of the function. We recall that a function f with two derivatives and
with f â€²(a)  0 has a relative maximum at a if its second derivative at a
is negative. It has a relative minimum at a if f â€²â€²(a) > 0. If f â€²â€²(a)  0, the
test fails. With the aid of Taylorâ€™s theorem for functions from RN into R1
we can establish the corresponding result for functions of N variables.

value a if there is a ball B(a, r) such that f(x) âˆ’f(a) â‰¤0 for x âˆˆB(a, r).

x âˆˆB(a, r) except for x  a. The corresponding deï¬nitions for local
minimum and strict local minimum reverse the inequality sign. If f
has partial derivatives at a, we say that f has a critical point at the value
a if Dif(a)  0, i  1, 2, . . . , N.

7.4
The Darboux Integral in RN
The development of the theory of integration in RN for N â‰¥2 parallels
the one-dimensional case given in Section 5.1. For functions deï¬ned on
an interval I in R1, we formed upper and lower sums by dividing I into a
number of subintervals. In RN we begin with a domain F, i.e., a bounded
region that has volume, and in order to form upper and lower sums,
we divide F into a number of subdomains. These subdomains are the

142
Differentiation and Integration in RN
generalizations of the subintervals in R1, and the limits of the upper and
lower sums as the number of subdomains tends to inï¬nity yield upper
and lower integrals. The subdomains can be thought of as hypercubes in
RN with the volume of each hypercube just N times the length of a side.
Deï¬nition
Let F be a bounded set in RN that is a domain. A subdivision of F is a
ï¬nite collection of domains {F1, F2, . . . , Fn} no two of which have common
interior points and the union of which is F. That is,
F  F1 âˆªF2 âˆª. . . âˆªFn,
Int(Fi) âˆ©Int(Fj)  âˆ…for i Ì¸ j.
We denote such a subdivision by the single letter 4.
Let D be a set in RN containing F and suppose that f : D â†’R1 is a
bounded function on F. Let 4 be a subdivision of F and set
Mi  sup f on Fi,
mi  inf f on Fi.
Deï¬nitions

formula
S+(f, 4) 
n

i1
MiV(Fi),
where V(Fi) is the volume in RN of the domain Fi. Similarly, the lower
sum of f is deï¬ned by
Sâˆ’(f, 4) 
n

i1
miV(Fi).
Let 4 be a subdivision of a domain F. Then 4â€², another subdivision of
F, is called a reï¬nement of 4 if every domain of 4â€² is entirely contained
in one of the domains of 4. Suppose that 41  {F1, F2, . . . , Fn} and 42 
{G1, G2, . . . , Gm} are two subdivisions of F. We say that 4â€², the subdivision

The Riemann Integral in RN
The method of extending the Riemann integral from R1 to RN is similar
to the extension of the Darboux integral described in Section 7.4.
Deï¬nitions
Let A be a set in a metric space S with metric d. We deï¬ne the diameter
of A as the sup d(x, y), where the supremum is taken over all x, y in A.
The notation diam A is used for the diameter of A.

Inï¬nite Series
8.1
Tests for Convergence and
Divergence
It is customary to use expressions such as
(8.1)
u1 + u2 + Â· Â· Â· + un + Â· Â· Â·
and
âˆ

n1
un
to represent inï¬nite series. The ui are called the terms of the series, and
the quantities
sn  u1 + u2 + Â· Â· Â· + un,
n  1, 2, . . . ,
are called the partial sums of the series. The symbols in (8.1) not only
deï¬ne an inï¬nite series but also are used as an expression for the sum
of the series when it converges. To avoid this ambiguity we deï¬ne an
inï¬nite series in terms of ordered pairs.
Deï¬nitions
An inï¬nite series is an ordered pair ({un}, {sn}) of inï¬nite sequences in
which sn  u1 + u2 + . . . + un for each n. The un are called the terms
of the series and the sn are called the partial sums. If there is a number
s such that sn â†’s as n â†’âˆ, we say that the series is convergent and
that the sum of the series is s. If the sn do not tend to a limit, we say that
the series is divergent.
150

8.1.
Tests for Convergence and Divergence
151
It is clear that an inï¬nite series is uniquely determined by the se-
quence {un} of its terms. There is almost never any confusion in using
the symbols in (8.1) for an inï¬nite series. While the deï¬nition in terms
of ordered pairs is satisfactory from the logical point of view, it does re-
quire a cumbersome notation. Rather than have unwieldy proofs that
may obscure their essential features, we shall use the standard notation
u1 + u2 + Â· Â· Â· + un + Â· Â· Â· and âˆ
n1 un to denote inï¬nite series. The context
will always show whether the expression represents the series itself or
the sum of the terms.

Theorem 8.7 (Alternating series theorem)
Suppose that the numbers un, n  1,2, . . ., satisfy the following conditions:
(i) The un are alternately positive and negative.
(ii) |un+1| < |un| for every n.
(iii) limnâ†’âˆun  0.
Then âˆ
n1 un is convergent. Furthermore, if the sum is denoted by s, then
s lies between the partial sums sn and sn+1 for each n.
Proof
Assume that u1 is positive. If not, consider the series beginning with u2,
since discarding one term does not affect convergence. With u1 > 0, we
have, clearly,
u2nâˆ’1 > 0 and u2n < 0 for all n.
We now write
s2n  (u1 + u2) + (u3 + u4) + . . . + (u2nâˆ’1 + u2n).
Since by (ii) above |u2k| < u2kâˆ’1 for each k, each term in parentheses is
positive, and so s2n increases with n. Also,
s2n  u1 + (u2 + u3) + (u4 + u5) + . . . + (u2nâˆ’2 + u2nâˆ’1) + u2n.
The terms in parentheses are negative, and u2n < 0. Therefore, s2n <
u1 for all n. Hence s2n is a bounded, increasing sequence and therefore
convergent to a number, say s; also, s2n â‰¤s for each n. By observing that
s2nâˆ’1  s2n âˆ’u2n, we have s2nâˆ’1 > s2n for all n. In particular, s2nâˆ’1 > s2 
u1 + u2, and so s2nâˆ’1 is bounded from below. Also,
s2n+1  s2nâˆ’1 + (u2n + u2n+1) < s2nâˆ’1.
Therefore, s2n+1 is a decreasing sequence that tends to a limit. Since u2n â†’
0 as n â†’âˆ, we see that s2n and s2n+1 tend to the same limit s. Because
s2nâˆ’1 is decreasing, it follows that s2nâˆ’1 â‰¥s for every n. Thus s â‰¤sp for
odd p and s â‰¥sp for even p.

A power series is a series of the form
c0 + c1(x âˆ’a) + c2(x âˆ’a)2 + . . . + cn(x âˆ’a)n + Â· Â· Â· ,

It will be established that most of the elementary functions such as the
trigonometric, logarithmic, and exponential functions have power series
expansions. In fact, power series may be used for the deï¬nition of many
of the functions studied thus far. For example, the function log x may be
deï¬ned by a power series rather than by an integral as in Section 5.3. If a
power series deï¬nition is used, the various properties of functions, such
as those given in Theorems 5.12 and 5.13 are usually more difï¬cult to
establish.
We ï¬rst state a lemma and then prove two theorems that establish the
basic properties of power series.

Theorem 8.11
Let âˆ

Proof

8.3
Uniform Convergence
Let {fn} be a sequence of functions with each function having a domain
containing an interval I of R1 and with range in R1. The convergence of
such a sequence may be examined at each value x in I. The concept of
uniform convergence, one that determines the nature of the convergence
of the sequence for all x in I, has many applications in analysis. Of special
interest is Theorem 8.12, which states that if all the fn are continuous, then
the limit function must be also.
Deï¬nition
We say the sequence {fn} converges uniformly on the interval I to the
function f if for each Îµ > 0 there is a number N independent of x such
that
(8.4)
|fn(x) âˆ’f(x)| < Îµ for all x âˆˆI and all n > N.
Uniform convergence differs from ordinary pointwise convergence in
that the integer N does not depend on x, although naturally it depends on
Îµ.
The geometric meaning of uniform convergence is illustrated in Figure
8.1. Condition (8.4) states that if Îµ is any positive number, then for n > N
the graph of y  fn(x) lies entirely below the graph of f(x) + Îµ and entirely
above the graph of f(x) âˆ’Îµ.
Figure 8.1 Illustrating uniform convergence.
It can happen that a sequence {fn(x)} converges to f(x) for each x on an
interval I but that the convergence is not uniform. For example, consider
the functions

The deï¬nition of uniform convergence is seldom a practical method
for deciding whether or not a speciï¬c sequence converges uniformly. The
importance of uniform convergence with regard to continuous functions
is illustrated in the next theorem.
Theorem 8.12
Suppose that fn, n  1,2, . . ., is a sequence of continuous functions on an
interval I and that {fn} converges uniformly to f on I. Then f is continuous
on I.

Many of the results on uniform convergence of sequences of functions
from R1 to R1 generalize directly to functions deï¬ned on a set A in a
metric space S and with range in R1.
Deï¬nition
Let A be a set in a metric space S and suppose that fn : S â†’R1,
n  1, 2, . . ., is a sequence of functions. The sequence {fn} converges
uniformly on A to a function f : A â†’R1 if for every Îµ > 0 there is a
number N such that
|fn(x) âˆ’f(x)| < Îµ for all x âˆˆA and all n > N.

Theorem 8.15

fn:S â†’R1. If {fn} converges uniformly to f on A, then f is continuous on A.
The proof is similar to the proof of Theorem 8.12.

Power Series
Let uk(x), k  1, 2, . . ., be functions deï¬ned on a set A in a metric space
S with range in R1.
Deï¬nition
The inï¬nite series âˆ
k1 uk(x) has the partial sums sn(x)  n
k1 uk(x).
The series is said to converge uniformly on a set A to a function s if
the sequence of partial sums {sn} converges uniformly to s on the set A.
The above deï¬nition shows that theorems on uniform convergence
of inï¬nite series may be reduced to corresponding results for uniform
convergence of sequences.
Theorem 8.18 (Analogue of Theorem 8.12)
Suppose that un, n  1,2, . . ., are continuous on a set A in a metric space S
and that âˆ
n1 un(x) converges uniformly on A to a function s(x). Then s is
continuous on A.
The proof of Theorem 8.18 as well as the proofs of the next two
theorems reduce to the proofs of the corresponding results for sequences.

In analogy with the convergence of inï¬nite series, it is important to

integrals converge and diverge.

10.1
Functions of Bounded Variation
In this section we introduce the notion of the variation of a function. This
quantity is useful for problems in physics, engineering, probability the-
ory, Fourier series, and many other subjects. However, our main purpose
here is to show how this concept can enlarge enormously the class of
functions that are integrable.

10.2
The Riemannâ€“Stieltjes Integral
We introduce a generalization of the Riemann integral, one in which a
function f is integrated with respect to a second function g. If g(x)  x,
then the generalized integral reduces to the Riemann integral. This new
integral, called the Riemannâ€“Stieltjes integral, has many applications not
only in various branches of mathematics, but in physics and engineering
as well. By choosing the function g appropriately we shall see that the
Riemannâ€“Stieltjes integral allows us to represent discrete as well as con-
tinuous processes in terms of integrals. This possibility yields applications
to probability theory and statistics.
