11.1
The Implicit Function Theorem
Suppose we are given a relation in R2 of the form
(11.1)
F(x, y)  0.
Then to each value of x there may correspond one or more values of y
that satisfy (11.1)â€”or there may be no values of y that do so. If I  {x :
x0 âˆ’h < x < x0 + h} is an interval such that for each x âˆˆI there is exactly
one value of y satisfying (11.1), then we say that F(x, y)  0 deï¬nes y as
a function of x implicitly on I. Denoting this function by f , we have
F[x, f(x)]  0 for x on I.
An implicit function theorem determines conditions under which a
relation such as (11.1) deï¬nes y as a function of x or x as a function of y.
The solution is a local one in the sense that the size of the interval I may
be much smaller than the domain of the relation F. Figure 11.1 shows the
graph of a relation such as (11.1). We see that F deï¬nes y as a function
of x in a region about P, but not beyond the point Q. Furthermore, the
relation does not yield y as a function of x in any region containing the
point Q in its interior.
The simplest example of an implicit function theorem states that if F
is smooth and if P is a point at which F,2 (that is, âˆ‚F/âˆ‚y) does not vanish,
then it is possible to express y as a function of x in a region containing
this point. More precisely, we have the following result.

increasing function of y. Furthermore, from (11.5) it follows that F(x, y) â†’
âˆ’âˆas y â†’âˆ’âˆ, and F(x, y) â†’+âˆas y â†’+âˆ. Since F is continuous,
for each ï¬xed x there is exactly one value of y such that F(x, y)  0.
Applying Theorem 11.1, we conclude that there is a function f on R1 that
is continuous and differentiable such that F[x, f(x)]  0 for all x.

11.2
Lagrange Multipliers
Let D be a region in Rm and suppose that f : D â†’R1 is a C1 function.
At any local maximum or minimum of f(x)  f(x1, x2, . . . , xm) we know
that f,i  0, i  1, 2, . . . , m. Solving these m equations in m unknowns
yields the critical points of f . Then there are second derivative tests to
determine which among these points are maxima, minima, or points of
inï¬‚ection. In many applications, especially in physics and engineering,
we seek not the pure maxima and minima of f , but the maximum or
minimum values of f when additional conditions are imposed. These are
usually called constraints. That is, the constraints are given by a set of
equations such as
(11.6)
Ï•1(x1, x2, . . . , xm)  0, Ï•2(x1, x2, . . . , xm)  0, . . . , Ï•k(x1, x2, . . . , xm)  0.
Equations (11.6) are called side conditions. There cannot be too many side
conditions. For example, if k were larger than m, then (11.6) may not have
any solutions at all. Therefore, we suppose generally that k is less than m,
and it is easy to see that the imposition of conditions (11.6) will change
the location of the critical points of f .
The method of Lagrange multipliers employs a simple technique for
determining the maxima and minima of f when equations (11.6) hold.
The method is especially useful when it is difï¬cult or not possible to ï¬nd
solutions of the system (11.6).
The Lagrange multiplier rule is frequently explained but seldom
proved. In Theorem 11.4 below we establish the validity of this rule, which
we now describe. We introduce k new variables (or parameters), denoted
by Î»  (Î»1, Î»2, . . . , Î»k), and we form the function of m + k variables
F(x, Î»)  F(x1, . . . , xm, Î»1, . . . , Î»k) â‰¡f(x) +
k

j1
Î»jÏ•j(x).
For this function F we compute the critical points when x is in D and Î» in
Rk without constraints. That is, we ï¬nd solutions to the m + k equations
formed by all the ï¬rst derivatives of F(x, Î»):
(11.7)

i  1, 2, . . . , m,
F,j  0,
j  1, 2, . . . , k.
We shall show that the critical points of the desired solution are among
the solutions of the system given by (11.7).

12.1
Vector Functions on RN
In this section we develop the basic properties of differential and integral
calculus for functions whose domain is a subset of RM and whose range
is in a vector space VN. We shall emphasize the coordinate-free character
of the deï¬nitions and results, but we will introduce coordinate systems
and use them in proofs and computations whenever convenient.
Deï¬nition
A vector function from a domain D in RM into VN is a mapping f :
D â†’VN. If the image is V1 (which we identify with R1 in the natural
way), then f is called a scalar function. Vector or scalar functions are
often called vector or scalar ï¬elds. The term ï¬eld is used because we
frequently visualize a vector function as superimposed on RM. At each
point of the domain D of RM a representative directed line segment of f
is drawn with base at that point. We obtain a ï¬eld of vectors.
Let f : D â†’VN be a vector function where D is a domain in RM. An
orthonormal basis for VN is a set of N unit vectors e1, e2, . . . , eN such
that ei Â· ej  0 for i Ì¸ j, i, j  1, 2, . . . , N. Then for each point P âˆˆD, we
may represent f by the formula

We can consider vector ï¬elds with domain in any space such as VM or EM
(Euclidean space). Equation (12.1) illustrates what is meant in that case.
We shall ordinarily assume that D is in RM. The function f is continuous
at P if each fi, i  1, 2, . . . , N, is continuous at P. We recall that the basic
properties of functions from RM into R1 were developed in Chapters 6
and 7. Properties developed there that involve linear processes are easily
transferred to functions f from RM into VN.
For simplicity we shall usually consider functions f from RN into VN,
although many of the results developed below are valid with only modest
changes if the dimension of the domain of f is different from that of its
range.
The deï¬nite integral of a vector function, deï¬ned below, is similar to
the integral of a scalar function on RN as given in Chapter 7.

Let I  {t : a â‰¤t â‰¤b} be an interval and f a vector function with
domain I and range D, a subset of VN. We consider in RN the directed
line segments having base at the origin 0 that represent the vectors f
in D. The heads of these directed line segments trace out a curve in RN,
which we denote by C. If f is continuous and the curve C has ï¬nite length,
then we say that C is a rectiï¬able path.
Let g be a continuous vector function from C into VN. If f, as deï¬ned
above, is rectiï¬able, then we can determine the Riemannâ€“Stieltjes integral
of g with respect to f. To do so we introduce a coordinate system with

Remark

evaluated by ï¬rst decomposing âˆ’â†’

If a smooth vector ï¬eld v is deï¬ned in a domain that is not merely

following result.

12.3
Greenâ€™s Theorem in the Plane
Greenâ€™s theorem is an extension to the plane of the Fundamental theorem
of calculus. We recall that this Fundamental theorem states that if I is an
interval in R1 and f : I â†’R1 is a continuously differentiable function
then for any points a and b in I we have the formula
(12.22)
 b
a
f â€²(x)dx  f(b) âˆ’f(a).
In the extension of this theorem to the plane we suppose that F is a
domain in R2 and C is its boundary. Then Greenâ€™s theorem is a formula
that connects the line integral of a vector function over C with the double
integral of the derivative of the function taken over the domain F.
Let D be a region in R2 with a boundary that is a simple closed curve.
We denote the unoriented boundary by âˆ‚D and the boundary oriented
in a counterclockwise direction by âˆ’â†’
âˆ‚D. The symbol âˆ’âˆ’â†’
âˆ‚D is used for the
clockwise orientation.
We shall ï¬rst establish Greenâ€™s theorem for regions in R2 that have a
special simple shape. Then we shall show how the result for these special
regions can be used to yield the same formula for general regions in the
plane.

Deï¬nitions
Let C1 and C2 be two smooth arcs that have as their only point in common
an endpoint of each of them. This point, denoted by P, is called a corner
of C1 and C2 if each of the arcs has a (one-sided) tangent line at P and if
the two tangent lines make a positive angle (see Figure 12.3). A piecewise
smooth simple closed curve is a simple closed curve that is made up
of a ï¬nite number of smooth arcs that are joined at corners.
In Lemma 12.3 we may assume that f is a piecewise continuously
differentiable (i.e., piecewise smooth) function on I consisting of a ï¬nite
number of smooth arcs joined at corners. The proof is the same.
Figure 12.3
Deï¬nition
A region D in R2 is said to be regular if (i) D is bounded, (ii) âˆ‚D consists
of a ï¬nite number of piecewise smooth simple closed curves, and (iii) at
each point P of âˆ‚D a Cartesian coordinate system can be constructed with
P as origin with the following properties: For sufï¬ciently small a and b,
a rectangle
R  {(x1, x2) : âˆ’a â‰¤x1 â‰¤a, âˆ’b â‰¤x2 â‰¤b}

on I  {x1 : âˆ’a â‰¤x1 â‰¤a} and with range on J  {x2 : âˆ’b < x2 < b},
where f is a piecewise smooth function (see Figure 12.4).
Of course, the values of a, b, and the function f will change with the
point P. If a rectangle R is determined for a point P, then it is clear that
any rectangle with the same value for b and a smaller value for a is also
adequate. See Figure 12.5 for examples of regions that are not regular.

Deï¬nitions
A smooth surface S is orientable if there exists a continuous unit normal
function deï¬ned over all of S. Such a unit normal function is called an
orientation of S.
Since the unit normal function to S at a point P is either n(P) or âˆ’n(P),
each orientable surface possesses exactly two orientations, each of which
is the negative of the other. An oriented surface is the pair (S, n), where
n is one of the two orientations of S. We denote such an oriented surface by
âˆ’â†’
S . Suppose that F is a smooth surface element of the oriented surface âˆ’â†’
S .

of F agrees with the orientation of S if the parametric representations of
F and S yield unit normal functions on F that are identical.

12.6
The Divergence Theorem
Greenâ€™s theorem establishes a relation between the line integral of a
function over the boundary of a plane region and the double integral
of the derivative of the same function over the region itself. Stokesâ€™s the-
orem extends this result to two-dimensional surfaces in three-space. In

254
12.
Vector Functions on RN; The Theorems of Green and Stokes
this section we establish another kind of generalization of the Funda-
mental theorem of calculus known as the Divergence theorem. This
theorem determines the relationship between an integral of the deriva-
tive of a function over a three-dimensional region in R3 and the integral
of the function itself over the boundary of that region. All three theorems
(Green, Stokes, Divergence) are special cases of a general formula that
connects an integral over a set of points in RN with another integral over
the boundary of that set of points. The integrand in the ï¬rst integral is a
certain derivative of the integrand in the boundary integral.
Let v  v1(x1, x2, x3)e1 + v2(x1, x2, x3)e2 + v3(x1, x2, x3)e3 be a vector
ï¬eld deï¬ned for (x1, x2, x3) in a region E in R3. We recall that div v is a
scalar ï¬eld given in coordinates by the formula
div v  âˆ‚v1
âˆ‚x1

+ âˆ‚v3
âˆ‚x3
.

holds generally, provided that the boundary of E is not too irregular and
that v is smooth.

The Divergence theorem will now be established for a wide class of
regions that are called â€œregular.â€ Intuitively, a region in R3 is regular if its
boundary can be subdivided into small pieces in such a way that each
piece has a smooth representation of the form x3  f(x1, x2) if a suitable
Cartesian coordinate system is introduced.
Deï¬nition
A region E in R3 is regular if:
(i) âˆ‚E consists of a ï¬nite number of smooth surfaces, each without
boundary;
(ii) at each point P of âˆ‚E a Cartesian coordinate system is introduced
with P as origin. There is a cylindrical domain H  {(x1, x2, x3) :
(x1, x2) âˆˆD, âˆ’âˆ< x3 < âˆ}, with D a region in the plane x3  0
containing the origin, that has the property that H âˆ©âˆ‚E is a surface
that can be represented in the form x3  f(x1, x2) for (x1, x2) âˆˆ
D âˆªâˆ‚D. Furthermore, f is smooth;
(iii) the set
H1  {(x1, x2, x3) : (x1, x2) âˆˆD, âˆ’c < x3 < f(x1, x2)}

If E is a regular region, then each point P of âˆ‚E is interior to a smooth
surface element except for those points on a ï¬nite number of arcs on âˆ‚E
that have zero surface area. If a line in the direction of the unit normal to
âˆ‚E is drawn through a point P interior to a smooth surface element on âˆ‚E,
then a segment of the line on one side of P will be in E, while a segment
on the other side will be exterior to E. Finally, we observe that n varies
continuously when P is in a smooth surface element of âˆ‚E.
